<span class="italic"><a title="Link to journal home page" href="https://doi.org/10.1039/2040-3372/2009">Nanoscale</a></span>, 2016, <strong>8</strong>, 17911-17918</div><h1 id="sect172"><span class="title_heading">Hierarchical porous nitrogen-rich carbon nanospheres with high and durable capabilities for lithium and sodium storage<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a></span></h1><p class="header_text">

      <br><a id="affa"><sup><span class="sup_ref italic">a</span></sup></a><span class="italic">Key Laboratory of Mesoscopic Chemistry of MOE and Collaborative Innovation Center of Chemistry for Life Sciences, School of Chemistry and Chemical Engineering, Nanjing University, Nanjing, Jiangsu 210093, China. E-mail: <a href="mailto:zhongjin@nju.edu.cn">zhongjin@nju.edu.cn</a>; <a href="mailto:j.liu@duke.edu">j.liu@duke.edu</a></span>

    </p><div id="art-admin"><span class="italic bold">Received 
      9th August 2016
    </span><span class="bold italic">, Accepted 22nd September 2016</span></div><p class="bold italic">First published on 23rd September 2016</p><hr><div class="abstract"><h2>Abstract</h2><p>To improve the energy storage performance of carbon-based materials, considerable attention has been paid to the design and fabrication of novel carbon architectures with structural and chemical modifications. Herein, we report that hierarchical porous nitrogen-rich carbon (HPNC) nanospheres originating from acidic etching of metal carbide/carbon hybrid nanoarchitectures can be employed as high-performance anode materials for both lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) and sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). The structural advantages of HPNC nanospheres are that the exceptionally-high content of nitrogen (17.4 wt%) can provide abundant electroactive sites and enlarge the interlayer distance (3.5 ) to improve the capacity, and the large amount of micropores and mesopores can serve as reservoirs for storing lithium/sodium ions. In LIBs, HPNC based anodes deliver a high reversible capacity of 1187 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> after 100 cycles at 100 mA g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, a great rate performance of 470 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> at 5000 mA g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, and outstanding cycling stabilities with a capacity of 788 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> after 500 cycles at 1000 mA g<small><sup>1</sup></small>. In SIBs, HPNC based anodes exhibit a remarkable reversible capacity of 357 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> at 100 mA g<small><sup>1</sup></small> and high long-term stability with a capacity of 136 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> after 500 cycles at 1000 mA g<small><sup>1</sup></small>.</p></div><hr>
    
      <h2 id="sect277"><span class="a_heading">Introduction</span></h2>
      <span>Rechargeable lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) are urgently desired as efficient energy supply systems for the widespread applications of portable electronics and electric vehicles,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit1"><sup><span class="sup_ref">13</span></sup></a> owing to their high specific capacity, rate capability and cycling stability. Graphite is commonly used as a commercial anode material for LIBs due to its high electrical conductivity and low cost, but the limited theoretical capacity (372 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>) cannot meet the increasing demands of society.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit4"><sup><span class="sup_ref">4</span></sup></a> To improve the energy storage performance, other IVA-group materials,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit5"><sup><span class="sup_ref">57</span></sup></a> metal oxides,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit8"><sup><span class="sup_ref">8,9</span></sup></a> sulfides<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit10"><sup><span class="sup_ref">10,11</span></sup></a>, carbides<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit12"><sup><span class="sup_ref">12,13</span></sup></a><span class="italic">etc.</span> have drawn much attention because of their large reversible capacities. However, the performance of existing anode materials is usually hindered by poor intrinsic conductivity and large capacity decay caused by the huge volume expansion during lithium insertion/desertion.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit14"><sup><span class="sup_ref">14,15</span></sup></a> Thus, carbon based materials are still playing a leading role as mainstream anode materials for commercial LIBs, and considerable attention has been directed towards the design of anode materials with rationally defined compositions and structures that could combine both high capacity and good stability.</span>
      <p class="otherpara">In addition, based on the earth-abundant element sodium from the alkaline family, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted considerable interest as a low-cost alternative to LIBs.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit16"><sup><span class="sup_ref">1618</span></sup></a> One of the major issues for the development of SIBs is the lack of suitable anode materials.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit18"><sup><span class="sup_ref">18,19</span></sup></a> Being highly conductive, chemically stable, eco-friendly and cost-effective, carbon nanomaterials are also regarded as a class of promising candidates among the limited choices of anode materials for SIBs. Therefore, it is quite attractive to develop a versatile carbon material with high performances viable for both LIBs and SIBs.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">To date, lots of carbon-based anode materials have been employed for LIBs/SIBs, such as hard carbons,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit20"><sup><span class="sup_ref">20,21</span></sup></a> carbon nanotubes,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit22"><sup><span class="sup_ref">22,23</span></sup></a> carbon nanofibers,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit24"><sup><span class="sup_ref">24,25</span></sup></a> carbon nanospheres,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit26"><sup><span class="sup_ref">26,27</span></sup></a> graphene<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit28"><sup><span class="sup_ref">28,29</span></sup></a> and so on. The nano-scale architectures of these materials are critically important for the energy storage performance, and high porosity is desirable to facilitate charge transfer and also beneficial to lithium/sodium storage.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit30"><sup><span class="sup_ref">30,31</span></sup></a> In particular, partly graphitized porous carbons are considered to be promising for LIBs/SIBs due to the appropriate porous structure and three-dimensional (3D) conductive matrix. For instance, hollow carbon nanospheres were applied as anode materials for LIBs, exhibiting a specific capacity of 630 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> after 50 cycles at a current density of 0.1 C.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit26"><sup><span class="sup_ref">26</span></sup></a> Recently, carbide-derived carbon (CDC) architectures are emerging as a novel class of carbon materials for providing tailored porous structures with facile adjustment of porosity and graphitization.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit32"><sup><span class="sup_ref">32,33</span></sup></a> Interestingly, it was reported that the structural control of CDC can provide a great increase of capacity under high-rate conditions and in prolonged cycling.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit34"><sup><span class="sup_ref">34</span></sup></a> Moreover, heteroatom doping (nitrogen, phosphorus, sulfur or boron, <span class="italic">etc.</span>) of porous carbon materials has been proven to enhance lithium/sodium storage performances.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit35"><sup><span class="sup_ref">35</span></sup></a> Among these heteroatom substitutions, nitrogen doping is particularly attractive because nitrogen has a similar atomic radius but a larger electronegativity compared to carbon, leading to an enhanced interaction with lithium/sodium ions.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit36"><sup><span class="sup_ref">36</span></sup></a> Besides, nitrogen doping can introduce different types of defects and vacancies, and thus provide more electroactive sites.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit37"><sup><span class="sup_ref">37,38</span></sup></a> However, high nitrogen content may influence the pore structure and electrical conductivity. Therefore, it still remains a challenge to build carbon nanostructures with an optimized porosity and appropriate elemental doping configuration.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">Herein, we report a facile and scalable approach to prepare carbide/carbon-derived hierarchical porous nitrogen-rich carbon (HPNC) nanospheres by using a ZnCo based Prussian blue analogue (PBA) and polyvinylpyrrolidone (PVP) as precursors. Through thermal annealing, the PBA/PVP precursor nanospheres can be converted to metal carbide/carbon hybrid nanoarchitectures, and then the HPNC nanospheres are finally produced by the subsequent HF etching. The as-obtained HPNC nanospheres have a hierarchical three-dimensional conductive structure containing a large number of mesopores and micropores, which can efficiently serve as reservoirs for lithium/sodium ions. Moreover, the content of nitrogen heteroatoms in the HPNC nanospheres is very high (17.4 wt%), so that plenty of electroactive sites were provided for adsorbing lithium/sodium ions. Compared to the previous results in the literature, the novel anodes based on HPNC nanospheres in this study exhibited excellent electrochemical performances in both LIBs (Table S1<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>)<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit24"><sup><span class="sup_ref">24,26,28,3952</span></sup></a> and SIBs (Table S2<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>).<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit23"><sup><span class="sup_ref">23,25,27,5362</span></sup></a></p>
    
    
      <h2 id="sect312"><span class="a_heading">Experimental</span></h2>
      
        <h3 id="sect314"><span class="b_heading">Chemicals</span></h3>
        <span>All chemicals in this work are of analytical purity and used without further purification.</span>
      
      
        <h3 id="sect317"><span class="b_heading">Synthesis of Zn<small><sub>3</sub></small>[Co(CN)<small><sub>6</sub></small>]<small><sub>2</sub></small><span class="italic">n</span>H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O/PVP precursor nanospheres</span></h3>
        <span>Typically, 0.60 mmol of zinc acetate dihydrate (Zn(Ac)<small><sub>2</sub></small>2H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O) and 0.40 g of polyvinylpyrrolidone (PVP) were dissolved in 20 mL of deionized water with ultrasonication to form a transparent solution. Then, 20 mL aqueous solution of 0.40 mmol potassium hexacyanocobaltate (K<small><sub>3</sub></small>[Co(CN)<small><sub>6</sub></small>]) was added into the above solution gradually under ultrasonication and vigorous stirring in an ice-water bath for 1 h. Then the mixture was quiescently aged for 6 h without any disturbance. The precipitate was then collected by centrifugation, washed with deionized water several times, and dried at 60 C in a vacuum oven.</span>
      
      
        <h3 id="sect329"><span class="b_heading">Synthesis of HPNC samples</span></h3>
        <span>To obtain the HPNC products, Zn<small><sub>3</sub></small>[Co(CN)<small><sub>6</sub></small>]<small><sub>2</sub></small><span class="italic">n</span>H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O/PVP precursor nanospheres were firstly thermally annealed for 2 h under a nitrogen atmosphere at designated temperature with a ramping rate of 2 C min<small><sup>1</sup></small>. Then, the resultant was etched by using hydrofluoric acid (HF, 5 wt%) and hydrochloric acid (HCl, 3.0 M) solution successively. The product was collected and washed with deionized water and absolute ethanol respectively several times, and dried at 60 C in a vacuum oven. The samples thermally annealed with the temperature of 500, 600, 700, and 800 C were denoted as HPNC-500, HPNC-600, HPNC-700, and HPNC-800, respectively.</span>
      
      
        <h3 id="sect338"><span class="b_heading">Instrumentation and characterization</span></h3>
        <span>Field-emission scanning electron microscopy (FESEM) images were collected on a JEOL JSM-6480 scanning electron microscope. Transmission electron microscopy (TEM, JEM-2100), scanning TEM (STEM) images and energy dispersive X-ray (EDX) spectroscopy mapping profiles were obtained on a JEOL JEM-2100F transmission electron microscope using an accelerating voltage of 200 kV. The dynamic light scattering (DLS) data were collected on a MasterSizer 2000 instrument. Powder X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns were recorded with an X-ray diffractometer (Bruker D-8 Advance) using Cu K (<span class="italic"></span> = 1.5406 ) radiation at a scanning rate of 6 min<small><sup>1</sup></small>. Raman scattering was carried out on a Horiba JY Raman spectrometer using a 473 nm laser source. Nitrogen sorption isotherms were obtained through BrunauerEmmettTeller (BET) analysis at 77 K on a Quantachrome Autosorb-IQ-2C-TCD-VP instrument. X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was performed on a PHI-5000 VersaProbe X-ray photoelectron spectrometer using Al K X-ray radiation. Fourier transform infrared (FT-IR) spectra were collected by using a Nicolet iS 10 spectrometer in the range of 4004000 cm<small><sup>1</sup></small>.</span>
      
      
        <h3 id="sect344"><span class="b_heading">Electrochemical measurements</span></h3>
        <span>The working electrodes were prepared by mixing the as-obtained HPNC sample, acetylene black and polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) binder in <span class="italic">N</span>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone (NMP) solvent with a weight ratio of 80<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">10<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">10. The mixture was stirred for 24 h and spread on a copper foil, and then dried in a vacuum oven at 60 C to remove the solvent. The obtained working electrode was assembled in a glove box filled with argon gas. The LIBs were assembled using a CR2032 cell with lithium metal foil as the counter electrode, and 1.0 M LiPF<small><sub>6</sub></small> in a mixture of ethylene carbonate and diethyl carbonate (1<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">1 in volume ratio) as the electrolyte. The SIBs were fabricated using sodium metal foil as the counter electrode, and 1.0 M NaClO<small><sub>4</sub></small> salt in propylene carbonate (PC) as the electrolyte. The electrochemical properties were tested on a Wuhan LAND battery test system. The loading mass of the active materials was <span class="italic">ca</span>. 0.60.9 mg, and the specific capacity was calculated based on the total weight of active materials.</span>
      
    
    
      <h2 id="sect351"><span class="a_heading">Results and discussion</span></h2>
      
        <h3 id="sect353"><span class="b_heading">Synthesis of HPNC nanospheres</span></h3>
        <span>An effective strategy was developed to prepare the HPNC nanospheres (Fig. S1<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>). Briefly, the steps of a typical preparation process include: (1) large-scale synthesis of PBA/PVP precursor nanospheres, (2) subsequent thermal annealing under a nitrogen gas flow to form metal carbide/carbon hybrid nanoarchitectures, and (3) acidic etching of metal carbides by hydrofluoric acid (HF, 5 wt%) and hydrochloric acid (HCl, 3.0 M) solution, successively. The PBA/PVP precursor nanospheres were synthesized by a solution-phase method using potassium hexacyanocobaltate (K<small><sub>3</sub></small>[Co(CN)<small><sub>6</sub></small>]), zinc acetate dihydrate (Zn(Ac)<small><sub>2</sub></small>2H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O) and PVP as starting materials. Fig. S2<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> shows the X-ray diffraction spectrum of PBA/PVP precursor nanospheres, all the diffraction peaks are attributed to the cubic phase of Zn<small><sub>3</sub></small>[Co(CN)<small><sub>6</sub></small>]<small><sub>2</sub></small><span class="italic">n</span>H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O (JCPDS card, no. 23-1494, space group: <span class="italic">Fm</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_0033_0304.gif" alt="[3 with combining macron]"><span class="italic">m</span>, <span class="italic">a</span> = 9.940, <span class="italic">b</span> = 9.940, <span class="italic">c</span> = 9.940). Field-emission scanning electron microscopy (FESEM) characterization (Fig. S3<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) revealed the spherical morphology of the Zn<small><sub>3</sub></small>[Co(CN)<small><sub>6</sub></small>]<small><sub>2</sub></small><span class="italic">n</span>H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O/PVP precursor with a narrow size distribution of about 500700 nm. After the annealing process, the resultant products inherited the globose shape of the Zn<small><sub>3</sub></small>[Co(CN)<small><sub>6</sub></small>]<small><sub>2</sub></small><span class="italic">n</span>H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O/PVP precursor and exhibited high surface roughness (Fig. S4<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>). Further transmission electron microscopy (TEM) characterization (Fig. S5<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) revealed that the samples after annealing consisted of numerous nanoparticles embedded into spherical carbon frameworks. XRD characterization (Fig. S6<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) demonstrated that the nanoparticles in HPNC samples were metal carbides, and with the increase of annealing temperature (from 500 to 800 C), the nanoparticles evolved from Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>ZnC to Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>C due to the gradual evaporation of Zn. In the last step, HPNC nanospheres were obtained by the etching of metal carbides in HF and HCl solutions successively. The final products were denoted as HPNC-<span class="italic">x</span> (<span class="italic">x</span> = 500, 600, 700, and 800), where <span class="italic">x</span> stands for the thermal annealing temperature.</span>
      
      
        <h3 id="sect391"><span class="b_heading">Characterization of HPNC nanospheres</span></h3>
        <span>The size, shape, and nanostructure of HPNC samples were examined by FESEM and transmission electron microscopy (TEM). <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1a and b</a> present the typical FESEM images of the HPNC-600 sample. The HPNC-600 product inherited the original spherical morphology of the Zn<small><sub>3</sub></small>[Co(CN)<small><sub>6</sub></small>]<small><sub>2</sub></small><span class="italic">n</span>H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O/PVP precursor. The size distribution of HPNC-600 nanospheres was obtained by DLS analysis (Fig. S7<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>); the slightly reduced size is attributed to the carbonization and volume shrinkage of Zn<small><sub>3</sub></small>[Co(CN)<small><sub>6</sub></small>]<small><sub>2</sub></small><span class="italic">n</span>H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O/PVP precursor nanospheres during the annealing step. The surface of HPNC-600 nanospheres became rougher after the annealing and etching steps (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1b</a>). TEM analysis was performed to investigate the surface and structural features of products. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1c</a> shows a typical magnified view of HPNC-600 nanospheres, displaying a hierarchical porous structure. The TEM image with higher magnification (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1d</a>) illustrated that the HPNC nanospheres possess lots of well-distributed mesopores. The mesopores were typically 510 nm in diameter and with a wall thickness of 34 nm (as marked with red arrows in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1d and e</a>). These pores were mainly generated by the etching of carbide nanoparticles embedded in the carbon frameworks. The corresponding schematic diagram of the porous structure of HPNC nanospheres is illustrated in the inset of <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1e</a>. The high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) images (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1f</a> and S8<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) taken at the edge of HPNC-600 nanospheres clearly show the presence of oriented multilayer graphitic sheets stacked in parallel with an adjacent interlayer distance of approximately 0.35 nm. This value is larger than the normal <span class="italic">d</span>-spacing of the (002) crystal plane of bulk graphite. The selected-area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern of the nanospheres in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1g</a> suggested the disordered nature of HPNC-600 nanospheres; the interlayer distance calculated from the SAED pattern is also 0.35 nm, well-consistent with the above HRTEM observation. The scanning TEM (STEM) image and energy-filtered TEM (EFTEM) elemental mapping (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1h and i</a>) revealed the co-existence of C, N, and O elements, and further demonstrated the homogeneous elemental distribution in HPNC-600 nanospheres. The morphological characteristics of HPNC-500, HPNC-700, and HPNC-800 samples were also investigated, as shown in Fig. S9.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> All the samples exhibited similar appearances and nanostructures to those of HPNC-600 nanospheres.</span>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig1"><a href="/image/article/2016/NR/c6nr06307a/c6nr06307a-f1_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c6nr06307a-f1.tif" src="/image/article/2016/NR/c6nr06307a/c6nr06307a-f1.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 1 </b> <span id="fig1"><span class="graphic_title">Morphology characterization of HPNC samples. (a, b) FESEM, (ce) TEM, (f) HRTEM, and (g) SAED images of HPNC-600 nanospheres. The red arrows in (d, e) indicate the presence of mesopores in HPNC-600 nanospheres. The inset of (e) is a schematic illustration of the porous structure of HPNC nanospheres. FESEM and TEM images reveal the uniform size distribution (400500 nm) of HPNC-600 nanospheres. The lattice distance of 0.35 nm in (f) corresponds to the (002) plane of graphite but with a slightly larger value, well-consistent with the SAED pattern in (g). (h) STEM image of a HPNC-600 nanosphere. (i) EFTEM image and the corresponding elemental mapping of C, N and O elements, respectively, demonstrating the homogeneous distribution of these elements.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <p class="otherpara">The XRD spectra of HPNC samples (HPNC-500, HPNC-600, HPNC-700, and HPNC-800) are presented in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2a</a>. These samples exhibited similar diffraction features with only a weak and broad diffraction peak at approximately 2<span class="italic"></span>  26, corresponding to carbon with partial graphitization.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit63"><sup><span class="sup_ref">63</span></sup></a> No other diffraction peak was observed from the XRD spectra, suggesting the complete etching of carbide nanoparticles in carbon frameworks. This result was further confirmed by the energy dispersive X-ray spectrometry (EDX) analysis shown in Fig. S10,<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> the existing elements were C, N and O, no Co or Zn was detected. The nitrogen species were derived from cyan groups (C<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_e002.gif" alt="[triple bond, length as m-dash]">N) and PVP in the Zn<small><sub>3</sub></small>[Co(CN)<small><sub>6</sub></small>]<small><sub>2</sub></small><span class="italic">n</span>H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O/PVP precursor nanospheres, while the small amount of the O element came from PVP. The Raman spectra of these HPNC samples (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2b</a>) displayed two Raman bands, which can be ascribed to the known D band (1355 cm<small><sup>1</sup></small>) and G band (1590 cm<small><sup>1</sup></small>), respectively. The G band is a characteristic feature of graphitic layers, while the D band corresponds to disordered carbon or defective graphitic structures. The intensity ratio of D to G band (<span class="italic">I</span><small><sub>D</sub></small>/<span class="italic">I</span><small><sub>G</sub></small>) is usually employed to evaluate the amounts of defects.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit64"><sup><span class="sup_ref">64,65</span></sup></a> The <span class="italic">I</span><small><sub>D</sub></small>/<span class="italic">I</span><small><sub>G</sub></small> of HPNC-600 (1.07) is higher than those of other samples (0.99 for HPNC-500, 0.98 for HPNC-700, and 0.94 for HPNC-800), suggesting the higher defect content generated during the annealing step. The specific surface areas and pore characteristics of the products were determined by nitrogen adsorptiondesorption isotherms, as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2c and d</a>. The BrunauerEmmettTeller (BET) surface areas (Table S3<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) were measured to be <span class="italic">ca</span>. 274.4, 531.5, 394.6 and 426.9 m<small><sup>2</sup></small> g<small><sup>1</sup></small> for HPNC-500, HPNC-600, HPNC-700, and HPNC-800 samples, respectively. Moreover, all samples exhibited both micropore (pore size centered at 1.0 nm) and mesopore (pore size between 315 nm) structures. According to previous studies,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit66"><sup><span class="sup_ref">66</span></sup></a> such a hierarchical pore distribution can facilitate the transfer of the electrolyte because the varying-sized pores and interconnections provide favorable pathways for ion penetration and diffusion.</p>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig2"><a href="/image/article/2016/NR/c6nr06307a/c6nr06307a-f2_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c6nr06307a-f2.tif" src="/image/article/2016/NR/c6nr06307a/c6nr06307a-f2.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 2 </b> <span id="fig2"><span class="graphic_title">Composition and structure characterization of HPNC samples. (a) XRD patterns, (b) Raman spectra, (c) nitrogen adsorptiondesorption isotherms and (d) pore size distributions of HPNC samples. (e, f) High-resolution XPS spectra of HPNC samples.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <p class="otherpara">X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was performed to investigate the compositions and chemical states of HPNC samples. The survey spectra (Fig. S11<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) suggested the co-existence of C, N, and O elements, consistent with the EDX results. The high-resolution XPS spectra of C 1s regions (Fig. S12<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) can be divided into three peaks. The main peak at 284.8 eV suggests that most of the carbon atoms are arranged in conjugated sp<small><sup>2</sup></small> honeycomb lattices. The weak peaks at 285.9 and 287.5 eV reflect different bonding structures of CN bonds, corresponding well to the Nsp<small><sup>2</sup></small> C and Nsp<small><sup>3</sup></small> C bonds, respectively.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit67"><sup><span class="sup_ref">67,68</span></sup></a> The high-resolution XPS spectra of N 1s regions (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2e and f</a>) can be deconvoluted into three peaks, pyridinic N (N-6, 398.4  0.2 eV), pyrrolic N (N-5, 399.8  0.2 eV) and graphitic N (400.7  0.4 eV). Based on the above analysis, a proposed structural scheme of a nitrogen-rich carbon layer in HPNC nanospheres is illustrated (Fig. S13<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>). Table S4<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> summarizes the parameters of XPS spectra and the fitting results of the N 1s peaks of HPNC samples. The nitrogen contents of HPNC-500, HPNC-600, HPNC-700, and HPNC-800 samples determined by XPS were <span class="italic">ca</span>. 19.3, 17.4, 10.6, and 5.1 wt%, respectively, comparable to the EDX results (Fig. S10<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>). The pyridinic and pyrrolic N atoms are the dominant N-containing species in the HPNC samples, which can serve as electroactive sites for enhancing the capacitive properties. Notably, the total atomic ratio of pyridinic and pyrrolic N in all N species decreased from 83.9% to 59.2% as the annealing temperature increased from 500 to 800 C, accompanied by the increasing graphitic N content from 16.1% to 40.8%, suggesting that an appropriate annealing temperature is very important to form carbon nanostructures with a favorable elemental doping configuration. Compared with the conventional post-treatment methods for preparing nitrogen-rich carbon analogues, the strategy in this work provides a facile and effective way for controllably introducing pyridinic and pyrrolic N into 3D porous carbon frameworks.</p>
      
      
        <h3 id="sect461"><span class="b_heading">Electrochemical performance of HPNC nanospheres</span></h3>
        <span>The HPNC nanospheres with unique structures and special compositions were expected to act as advanced anode materials for LIBs. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3a</a> shows the typical cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of a HPNC-600 based anode within the potential range of 0.013.0 V <span class="italic">vs</span>. Li/Li<small><sup>+</sup></small> at a scan rate of 0.2 mV s<small><sup>1</sup></small>. A strong peak at 0.6 V was observed in the first cycle and disappeared in the subsequent cycles, suggesting the occurrence of some irreversible side reactions on the surface of the electrode and the formation of solid-electrolyte interface (SEI) films.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit39"><sup><span class="sup_ref">39,69</span></sup></a> From the 2<small><sup>nd</sup></small> cycle onwards, the CV curves of the HPNC-600 based anode are almost overlapped, indicating high stability and superior reversibility. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3b</a> displays the typical chargedischarge profiles of the HPNC-600 based anode for the 1<small><sup>st</sup></small>, 2<small><sup>nd</sup></small>, 50<small><sup>th</sup></small>, and 100<small><sup>th</sup></small> cycles at 100 mA g<small><sup>1</sup></small> between 0.013.0 V <span class="italic">vs</span>. Li/Li<small><sup>+</sup></small>. The chargedischarge profiles of HPNC-600 are in accordance with those of carbon-based LIB anode materials in the literature.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit40"><sup><span class="sup_ref">40,41</span></sup></a> The CV curves and typical chargedischarge profiles of other HPNC samples (HPNC-500, HPNC-700 and HPNC-800 nanospheres) are presented in Fig. S14,<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> showing similar characteristics to those observed from HPNC-600 nanospheres. The first discharge and charge capacities of the HPNC-600 based anode are 1972 and 1129 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, respectively, corresponding to a moderate Coulombic efficiency of 57.2%. The capacity loss is ascribed to the formation of SEI films on the surface of active materials. The reversible discharge capacity at the 50<small><sup>th</sup></small> and 100<small><sup>th</sup></small> cycle is as high as 1197 and 1187 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, respectively, which is partly attributed to the ultrahigh contents of pyridinic and pyrrolic N in the HPNC-600 nanospheres. Remarkably, the reversible capacity of the HPNC-600 based anode is higher than those of the reported pristine or nitrogen-doped carbon anode materials (Table S1<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>),<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit24"><sup><span class="sup_ref">24,26,28,3952</span></sup></a> further demonstrating the excellent lithium storage performance of HPNC samples.</span>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig3"><a href="/image/article/2016/NR/c6nr06307a/c6nr06307a-f3_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c6nr06307a-f3.tif" src="/image/article/2016/NR/c6nr06307a/c6nr06307a-f3.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 3 </b> <span id="fig3"><span class="graphic_title">Lithium storage performance of HPNC samples. (a) CV curves of the HPNC-600 based anode at a scan rate of 0.2 mV s<small><sup>1</sup></small>. (b) The typical chargedischarge profiles and (c) cycling performance of HPNC-600 based anode at 100 mA g<small><sup>1</sup></small>. (d) Rate performance of the HPNC-600 based anode at various current densities. (e) Long-term cycling performances of the HPNC-600 based anode at 1000 and 5000 mA g<small><sup>1</sup></small>.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <p class="otherpara">The cycling performance of HPNC-600 nanospheres was firstly evaluated for 100 cycles at 100 mA g<small><sup>1</sup></small> (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3c</a>). The HPNC-600 based anode showed excellent cycling stability with a high discharge capacity in the first 100 cycles. The discharge capacity of the 2<small><sup>nd</sup></small> cycle reached 1133 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>. After 100 cycles, the discharge capacity is 1187 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, which is approximately 104.7% to that of the 2<small><sup>nd</sup></small> cycle. The cycling performance of other HPNC samples was also investigated (Fig. S15<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>), exhibiting high cycling stability, but with discharge capacity lower than HPNC-600. After 100 cycles, the discharge capacity of HPNC-500, HPNC-700, and HPNC-800 was 755, 499, and 384 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, respectively. Moreover, in comparison with other carbon-based anode materials in the literature (Table S1<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>),<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit24"><sup><span class="sup_ref">24,26,28,3952</span></sup></a> the HPNC-600 based anode also displayed much better cycling performance.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">The rate performance of HPNC samples was also investigated. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3d</a> shows the chargedischarge capacities of HPNC-600 nanospheres at various current densities from 100 to 5000 mA g<small><sup>1</sup></small>. The discharge capacities at 100, 200, 500, 1000, 2000, and 5000 mA g<small><sup>1</sup></small> were 1158, 1033, 907, 796, 668, and 470 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, respectively. When the current density was restored to 1000 mA g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, the discharge capacity recovered to 789 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, indicating high rate capability. For comparison, the rate capabilities of HPNC-500, HPNC-700, and HPNC-800 based anodes are displayed in Fig. S16,<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> showing comparable rate capabilities but with relatively lower specific capacities compared to HPNC-600. Therefore, it can be concluded that the HPNC-600 nanospheres exhibit the best electrochemical performance among all of the HPNC samples, owing to the high nitrogen content (especially the pyridinic (N-6) and pyrrolic (N-5) nitrogen species), low graphitic N content,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit42"><sup><span class="sup_ref">42</span></sup></a> and high specific surface area. Although the nitrogen content of HPNC-500 is higher than HPNC-600, the electrochemical performance is relatively lower. This difference can be undoubtedly attributed to the annealing temperature (500 C), which is too low and not optimal for the carbonization/graphitization of PBA/PVP precursor nanospheres. As shown in the Fourier transform infrared (FT-IR) spectra (Fig. S17<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>), some nitrogen atoms in the HPNC-500 sample may still exist in the form of C<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_e002.gif" alt="[triple bond, length as m-dash]">N groups, which is not beneficial to lithium storage. The resistance characteristics of HPNC samples were also investigated by the Nyquist plots (Fig. S18<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>); all of the HPNC based anodes displayed low charge and transfer resistances, owing to the high electrical conductivity.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">The cycling performances of HPNC samples at a high current density (1000 mA g<small><sup>1</sup></small>) were measured. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3e</a> shows the long-term cycling performance of the HPNC-600 based anode within the potential range of 0.013.0 V <span class="italic">vs</span>. Li/Li<small><sup>+</sup></small>. The reversible discharge capacity at the 2<small><sup>nd</sup></small> cycle was 1018 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, and still maintained at 788 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> even after 500 cycles. This corresponds to a high capacity retention of 77.4% compared to the 2<small><sup>nd</sup></small> cycle, suggesting extraordinary cycling stability. Moreover, the corresponding Coulombic efficiency of the HPNC-600 based anode in the long-term test (Fig. S19<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) is close to 100%. The cycling performances of other HPNC samples at 1000 mA g<small><sup>1</sup></small> are provided in Fig. S20,<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> exhibiting a comparable cycling performance but with a lower specific capacity than HPNC-600 after 500 cycles (316 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> for HPNC-500, 425 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> for HPNC-700, and 272 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> for HPNC-800, respectively). In addition, the initial Coulombic efficiency of the HPNC-600 based anode was about 45.2%, much higher than those of other HPNC anodes (29.5% for HPNC-500, 34.1% for HPNC-700, and 23.6% for HPNC-800, respectively).</p>
        <p class="otherpara">As the current density further increased to 5000 mA g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, the HPNC-600 based anode still exhibited excellent cycling performance. As shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3e</a>, the specific capacity of the HPNC-600 based anode increased in the first 50 cycles (from 467 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> at the 2<small><sup>nd</sup></small> cycle to 497 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> at the 50<small><sup>th</sup></small> cycle), then it decreased very slowly and still delivered a specific capacity of 396 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> after 1000 cycles (with a capacity retention of <span class="italic">ca</span>. 84.4% compared to the 2<small><sup>nd</sup></small> cycle). To verify the structural integrity of HPNC materials, the morphology of HPNC-600 nanospheres after long-term stability tests at 5000 mA g<small><sup>1</sup></small> was investigated. As shown in Fig. S21,<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> the SEM and further TEM characterization revealed that the morphology and structure of HPNC-600 nanospheres preserved well even after 1000 consecutive cycles at a very high current density, indicating the high stability and structural integrity of HPNC materials.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">Different from lithium storage, the sodium storage performance of carbon materials can be greatly influenced by the interlayer distance, as illustrated in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4</a>. Owing to the very small size (0.76 ), lithium ions can be easily inserted into and extracted from pristine carbon materials. When nitrogen-doping was introduced into pristine carbon materials, the lithium storage performance can be only slightly improved, mainly owing to the increased amounts of electroactive sites. However, the storage of sodium ions in carbon-based materials is usually restricted by the limited interlayer distance of the graphitic structure (3.35 ).<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit18"><sup><span class="sup_ref">18</span></sup></a> By introducing nitrogen heteroatoms into carbon materials, the insertion/extraction of sodium ions become much easier, due to the enlarged interlayer distance (3.5  in this work) and the increased amounts of electroactive sites and structural defects. Therefore, the enhancement effect of nitrogen doping on the sodium storage capacity is much stronger than that on the lithium storage capacity.</p>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig4"><a href="/image/article/2016/NR/c6nr06307a/c6nr06307a-f4_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c6nr06307a-f4.tif" src="/image/article/2016/NR/c6nr06307a/c6nr06307a-f4.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 4 </b> <span id="fig4"><span class="graphic_title">Schematic diagrams of the lithium and sodium storage behaviors in pristine or nitrogen-rich carbon materials.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <p class="otherpara">
          <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5a</a> shows the CV curves of the HPNC-600 based anode at 100 mA g<small><sup>1</sup></small> within the potential window of 0.013.0 V <span class="italic">vs</span>. Na/Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>. Similar to LIBs, the capacity loss at the 2<small><sup>nd</sup></small> cycle was ascribed to the formation of SEI films, and the overlap of the 2<small><sup>nd</sup></small>, 3<small><sup>rd</sup></small>, and 4<small><sup>th</sup></small> cycles suggested the high stability of the HPNC anode.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit70"><sup><span class="sup_ref">70</span></sup></a> Typical chargedischarge curves of the HPNC-600 based anode at 100 mA g<small><sup>1</sup></small> are presented in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5b</a>. The first discharge and charge capacities were 788 and 471 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, respectively, corresponding to a Coulombic efficiency of 59.8%. The cycling performance of the HPNC-600 based anode at 100 mA g<small><sup>1</sup></small> is presented in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5c</a>, exhibiting a high stability within the first 100 cycles. After 100 cycles, the discharge capacity reached 272 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, and the capacity retention measured from the 2<small><sup>nd</sup></small> cycle was <span class="italic">ca</span>. 71.2%. Compared with other HPNC samples (Fig. S22<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>), the HPNC-600 based anode delivered the highest specific capacity. The rate performance of the HPNC-600 based anode was also examined (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5d</a>), showing good rate performance with the capacities of 357, 292, 237, and 184 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> at 100, 200, 500 and 1000 mA g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, respectively. When the current density was restored to 100 mA g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, a high specific capacity of 327 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> was obtained. The long-term cycling performance of the HPNC-600 based anode for SIBs at 1000 mA g<small><sup>1</sup></small> is presented in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5e</a>. The specific capacity of the 2<small><sup>nd</sup></small> cycle was 231 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, and reached 136 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> at the 500<small><sup>th</sup></small> cycle, indicating the great cycling performance of HPNC samples for SIBs. The high structural integrity of the HPNC sample after long-term cycling tests was further confirmed by FESEM, as shown in Fig. S23.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> Compared with other pristine or nitrogen-doped carbon materials for SIBs (Table S2<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>),<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit23"><sup><span class="sup_ref">23,25,27,5362</span></sup></a> the as-prepared HPNC material exhibits remarkable sodium storage performance, suggesting its potential as promising anode materials for SIBs.</p>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig5"><a href="/image/article/2016/NR/c6nr06307a/c6nr06307a-f5_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c6nr06307a-f5.tif" src="/image/article/2016/NR/c6nr06307a/c6nr06307a-f5.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 5 </b> <span id="fig5"><span class="graphic_title">Sodium storage performance of HPNC samples. (a) CV curves of the HPNC-600 based SIB anode at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<small><sup>1</sup></small>. (b) Typical chargedischarge profiles and (c) cycling performance of the HPNC-600 based SIB anode at 100 mA g<small><sup>1</sup></small>. (d) Rate performance of the HPNC-600 based SIB anode at various current densities. (e) Long-term cycling performance of the HPNC-600 based SIB anode at 1000 mA g<small><sup>1</sup></small>.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <p class="otherpara">The superior lithium/sodium storage performances of HPNC nanospheres are attributed to the unique compositions and structure, which bring about the following advantages: (1) the hierarchical pore distribution (micropores and mesopores) can facilitate the transfer of the electrolyte because the varying-sized pores and interconnections provide more favorable pathways for ion penetration and diffusion, and the pores can also serve as reservoirs for lithium/sodium ions;<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit30"><sup><span class="sup_ref">30,31</span></sup></a> (2) the introduction of high-content pyridinic and pyrrolic N into the carbon frameworks can induce more electroactive sites and enlarge the interlayer distance (3.5  in this work) to further improve the capacity;<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit37"><sup><span class="sup_ref">37,38</span></sup></a> (3) the three-dimensional carbon scaffold of HPNC nanospheres can provide a highly conductive network for electron transfer, and is also very helpful to stabilize the as-formed SEI films on the surface.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit71"><sup><span class="sup_ref">71,72</span></sup></a> As a result, the synergistic effect of these factors leads to excellent lithium/sodium storage performances of HPNC nanospheres.</p>
      
    
    
      <h2 id="sect587"><span class="a_heading">Conclusions</span></h2>
      <span>In summary, we rationally designed and prepared carbide/carbon derived HPNC nanospheres by thermal annealing and following acidic etching of PBA/PVP precursor nanospheres. The high content of the nitrogen species, hierarchical pore structure and three-dimensional conductive scaffolds of HPNC materials significantly improved the lithium and sodium storage performance. The HPNC material is a promising candidate as an anode material for both LIBs and SIBs, and is also expected to find broad applications in other energy conversion and storage fields.</span>

They are usually only set in response to actions made by you which amount to a request for services, such as setting your privacy preferences, logging in or filling in forms. You can set your browser to block or alert you about these cookies, but some parts of the site will not then work. These cookies do not store any personally identifiable information.</p></div></div><div class="ot-accordion-layout ot-cat-item ot-vs-config" data-optanongroupid="C0002"><button aria-expanded="false" ot-accordion="true" aria-controls="ot-desc-id-C0002" aria-labelledby="ot-header-id-C0002"></button><!-- Accordion header --><div class="ot-acc-hdr"><div class="ot-plus-minus"><span></span><span></span></div><h4 class="ot-cat-header" id="ot-header-id-C0002">Analytical cookies</h4><div class="ot-tgl"><input type="checkbox" name="ot-group-id-C0002" id="ot-group-id-C0002" role="switch" class="category-switch-handler" data-optanongroupid="C0002" aria-labelledby="ot-header-id-C0002"> <label class="ot-switch" for="ot-group-id-C0002"><span class="ot-switch-nob"></span> <span class="ot-label-txt">Analytical cookies</span></label> </div></div><!-- accordion detail --><div class="ot-acc-grpcntr ot-acc-txt"><p class="ot-acc-grpdesc ot-category-desc" id="ot-desc-id-C0002">These cookies allow us to count visits and traffic sources so we can measure and improve the performance of our site. They help us to know which pages are the most and least popular and see how visitors move around the site. All information these cookies collect is aggregated and therefore anonymous. If you do not allow these cookies we will not know when you have visited our site, and will not be able to monitor its performance.</p></div></div><div class="ot-accordion-layout ot-cat-item ot-vs-config" data-optanongroupid="C0003"><button aria-expanded="false" ot-accordion="true" aria-controls="ot-desc-id-C0003" aria-labelledby="ot-header-id-C0003"></button><!-- Accordion header --><div class="ot-acc-hdr"><div class="ot-plus-minus"><span></span><span></span></div><h4 class="ot-cat-header" id="ot-header-id-C0003">Functional cookies</h4><div class="ot-tgl"><input type="checkbox" name="ot-group-id-C0003" id="ot-group-id-C0003" role="switch" class="category-switch-handler" data-optanongroupid="C0003" aria-labelledby="ot-header-id-C0003"> <label class="ot-switch" for="ot-group-id-C0003"><span class="ot-switch-nob"></span> <span class="ot-label-txt">Functional cookies</span></label> </div></div><!-- accordion detail --><div class="ot-acc-grpcntr ot-acc-txt"><p class="ot-acc-grpdesc ot-category-desc" id="ot-desc-id-C0003">These cookies enable the website to provide enhanced functionality and personalisation. They may be set by us or by third party providers whose services we have added to our pages. If you do not allow these cookies then some or all of these services may not function properly.</p></div></div><div class="ot-accordion-layout ot-cat-item ot-vs-config" data-optanongroupid="C0004"><button aria-expanded="false" ot-accordion="true" aria-controls="ot-desc-id-C0004" aria-labelledby="ot-header-id-C0004"></button><!-- Accordion header --><div class="ot-acc-hdr"><div class="ot-plus-minus"><span></span><span></span></div><h4 class="ot-cat-header" id="ot-header-id-C0004">Targeting cookies</h4><div class="ot-tgl"><input type="checkbox" name="ot-group-id-C0004" id="ot-group-id-C0004" role="switch" class="category-switch-handler" data-optanongroupid="C0004" aria-labelledby="ot-header-id-C0004"> <label class="ot-switch" for="ot-group-id-C0004"><span class="ot-switch-nob"></span> <span class="ot-label-txt">Targeting cookies</span></label> </div></div><!-- accordion detail --><div class="ot-acc-grpcntr ot-acc-txt"><p class="ot-acc-grpdesc ot-category-desc" id="ot-desc-id-C0004">These cookies may be set through our site by our advertising partners. They may be used by those companies to build a profile of your interests and show you relevant adverts on other sites. They do not store directly personal information, but are based on uniquely identifying your browser and internet device. If you do not allow these cookies, you will experience less targeted advertising.</p></div></div><div class="ot-accordion-layout ot-cat-item ot-vs-config" data-optanongroupid="BG58"><button
